<!--Polyfills CustomEvent constructor in IE. Allows us to use events to manage race conditions in client side js-->
<script>
    (function () {
        if ( typeof window.CustomEvent === "function" ) return false;
        function CustomEvent ( event, params ) {
            params = params || { bubbles: false, cancelable: false, detail: null };
            var evt = document.createEvent( 'CustomEvent' );
            evt.initCustomEvent( event, params.bubbles, params.cancelable, params.detail );
            return evt;
        }

        CustomEvent.prototype = window.Event.prototype;

        window.CustomEvent = CustomEvent;
    })();
</script>

    <meta name="journal_id" content="41588"/>

    <meta name="dc.title" content="European maize genomes highlight intraspecies variation in repeat and gene content"/>

    <meta name="dc.source" content="Nature Genetics 2020 52:9"/>

    <meta name="dc.format" content="text/html"/>

    <meta name="dc.publisher" content="Nature Publishing Group"/>

    <meta name="dc.date" content="2020-07-27"/>

    <meta name="dc.type" content="OriginalPaper"/>

    <meta name="dc.language" content="En"/>

    <meta name="dc.copyright" content="2020 The Author(s)"/>

    <meta name="dc.rightsAgent" content="journalpermissions@springernature.com"/>

    <meta name="dc.description" content="The diversity of maize (Zea mays) is the backbone of modern heterotic patterns and hybrid breeding. Historically, US farmers exploited this variability to establish today&#8217;s highly productive Corn Belt inbred lines from blends of dent and flint germplasm pools. Here, we report de novo genome sequences of four European flint lines assembled to pseudomolecules with scaffold N50 ranging from 6.1 to 10.4&#8201;Mb. Comparative analyses with two US Corn Belt lines explains the pronounced differences between both germplasms. While overall syntenic order and consolidated gene annotations reveal only moderate pangenomic differences, whole-genome alignments delineating the core and dispensable genome, and the analysis of heterochromatic knobs and orthologous long terminal repeat retrotransposons unveil the dynamics of the maize genome. The high-quality genome sequences of the flint pool complement the maize pangenome and provide an important tool to study maize improvement at a genome scale and to enhance modern hybrid breeding. De novo genome assemblies of four European flint maize lines and comparison with two US Corn Belt genomes provide insights into the dynamics of intraspecies variation in repeat and gene content in maize genomes."/>

class="u-js-hide">¬†
            <a class="js-orcid" itemprop="url" href="http://orcid.org/0000-0002-5155-0884"><span class="u-visually-hidden">ORCID: </span>orcid.org/0000-0002-5155-0884</a></span><sup class="u-js-hide"><a href="#Aff3">3</a><span itemprop="affiliation" itemscope="itemscope" itemtype="http://schema.org/Organization" class="u-visually-hidden"><meta itemprop="name" content="Institute for Crop Science and Resource Conservation, University of Bonn" /><meta itemprop="address" content="0000 0001 2240 3300, grid.10388.32, Crop Functional Genomics, Institute for Crop Science and Resource Conservation, University of Bonn, Bonn, Germany" /></span></sup>, </li><li class="c-author-list__item" itemprop="author" itemscope="itemscope" itemtype="http://schema.org/Person"><span itemprop="name"><a data-test="author-name" data-track="click" data-track-action="open author" data-track-label="link" href="#auth-Milena-Ouzunova" data-author-popup="auth-Milena-Ouzunova">Milena Ouzunova</a></span><sup class="u-js-hide"><a href="#Aff5">5</a><span itemprop="affiliation" itemscope="itemscope" itemtype="http://schema.org/Organization" class="u-visually-hidden"><meta itemprop="name" content="KWS SAAT SE" /><meta itemprop="address" content="grid.425691.d, KWS SAAT SE, Einbeck, Germany" /></span></sup>, </li><li class="c-author-list__item" itemprop="author" itemscope="itemscope" itemtype="http://schema.org/Person"><span itemprop="name"><a data-test="author-name" data-track="click" data-track-action="open author" data-track-label="link" href="#auth-Andreas-Houben" data-author-popup="auth-Andreas-Houben">Andreas Houben</a></span><span class="u-js-hide">¬†
            <a class="js-orcid" itemprop="url" href="http://orcid.org/0000-0003-3419-239X"><span class="u-visually-hidden">ORCID: </span>orcid.org/0000-0003-3419-239X</a></span><sup class="u-js-hide"><a href="#Aff4">4</a><span itemprop="affiliation" itemscope="itemscope" itemtype="http://schema.org/Organization" class="u-visually-hidden"><meta itemprop="name" content="Leibniz Institute of Plant Genetics and Crop Plant Research" /><meta itemprop="address" content="0000 0001 0943 9907, grid.418934.3, Leibniz Institute of Plant Genetics and Crop Plant Research, Seeland, Germany" /></span></sup>, </li><li class="c-author-list__item" itemprop="author" itemscope="itemscope" itemtype="http://schema.org/Person"><span itemprop="name"><a data-test="author-name" data-track="click" data-track-action="open author" data-track-label="link" href="#auth-Chris_Carolin-Sch_n" data-author-popup="auth-Chris_Carolin-Sch_n" data-corresp-id="c1">Chris-Carolin Sch√∂n<svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-email"></use></svg></a></span><span class="u-js-hide">¬†
            <a class="js-orcid" itemprop="url" href="http://orcid.org/0000-0001-6067-7900"><span class="u-visually-hidden">ORCID: </span>orcid.org/0000-0001-6067-7900</a></span><sup class="u-js-hide"><a href="#Aff2">2</a><span itemprop="affiliation" itemscope="itemscope" itemtype="http://schema.org/Organization" class="u-visually-hidden"><meta itemprop="name" content="Technical University Munich" /><meta itemprop="address" content="0000000123222966, grid.6936.a, Plant Breeding, School of Life Sciences, Technical University Munich, Munich, Germany" /></span></sup> &amp; </li><li class="c-author-list__item" itemprop="author" itemscope="itemscope" itemtype="http://schema.org/Person"><span itemprop="name"><a data-test="author-name" data-track="click" data-track-action="open author" data-track-label="link" href="#auth-Klaus_F__X_-Mayer" data-author-popup="auth-Klaus_F__X_-Mayer" data-corresp-id="c2">Klaus F. X. Mayer<svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-email"></use></svg></a></span><span class="u-js-hide">¬†
            <a class="js-orcid" itemprop="url" href="http://orcid.org/0000-0001-6484-1077"><span class="u-visually-hidden">ORCID: </span>orcid.org/0000-0001-6484-1077</a></span><sup class="u-js-hide"><a href="#Aff1">1</a>,<a href="#Aff6">6</a><span itemprop="affiliation" itemscope="itemscope" itemtype="http://schema.org/Organization" class="u-visually-hidden"><meta itemprop="name" content="Helmholtz Center Munich" /><meta itemprop="address" content="0000 0004 0483 2525, grid.4567.0, Plant Genome and Systems Biology, Helmholtz Center Munich, Neuherberg, Germany" /></span><span itemprop="affiliation" itemscope="itemscope" itemtype="http://schema.org/Organization" class="u-visually-hidden"><meta itemprop="name" content="Technical University Munich" /><meta itemprop="address" content="0000000123222966, grid.6936.a, School of Life Sciences, Technical University Munich, Munich, Germany" /></span></sup>¬†</li></ul>

            <div class="c-article-body">
                <section aria-labelledby="Abs1" data-title="Abstract" lang="en"><div class="c-article-section" id="Abs1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Abs1">Abstract</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Abs1-content"><p>The diversity of maize (<i>Zea mays</i>) is the backbone of modern heterotic patterns and hybrid breeding. Historically, US farmers exploited this variability to establish today‚Äôs highly productive Corn Belt inbred lines from blends of dent and flint germplasm pools. Here, we report de novo genome sequences of four European flint lines assembled to pseudomolecules with scaffold N50 ranging from 6.1 to 10.4‚ÄâMb. Comparative analyses with two US Corn Belt lines explains the pronounced differences between both germplasms. While overall syntenic order and consolidated gene annotations reveal only moderate pangenomic differences, whole-genome alignments delineating the core and dispensable genome, and the analysis of heterochromatic knobs and orthologous long terminal repeat retrotransposons unveil the dynamics of the maize genome. The high-quality genome sequences of the flint pool complement the maize pangenome and provide an important tool to study maize improvement at a genome scale and to enhance modern hybrid breeding.</p></div></div></section>
                <noscript>
                    
                </noscript>

                    </div>
                
                
                    <section aria-labelledby="Sec1" data-title="Main"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec1">Main</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec1-content"><p>Since its domestication ~10,000 years ago by Native Americans, maize (<i>Zea mays</i> ssp. <i>mays</i>) has become one of the most important sources for human nutrition and animal feeding. Extensive variation in landraces and breeding germplasm such as dent or flint corns underpins the enormous phenotypic and genetic diversity of maize<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Doebley, J. F., Goodman, O. M. &amp; Stuber, C. W. Exceptional genetic divergence of northern flint corn. Am. J. Bot. 73, 64‚Äì69 (1986)." href="#ref-CR1" id="ref-link-section-d28078e626">1</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Liu, K. et al. Genetic structure and diversity among maize inbred lines as inferred from DNA microsatellites. Genetics 165, 2117‚Äì2128 (2003)." href="#ref-CR2" id="ref-link-section-d28078e626_1">2</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 3" title="Chia, J. M. et al. Maize HapMap2 identifies extant variation from a genome in flux. Nat. Genet 44, 803‚Äì807 (2012)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0671-9#ref-CR3" id="ref-link-section-d28078e629">3</a></sup>. Today, US hybrids produced from inbred lines of different heterotic groups; for example, stiff stalk and nonstiff stalk<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 4" title="Duvick, D. N. The contribution of breeding to yield advances in maize (Zea mays L.). Adv. Agron. 86, 83‚Äì145 (2005)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0671-9#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d28078e633">4</a></sup> are highly productive and agriculturally important worldwide. These US Corn Belt dents resulted from crosses in the nineteenth century between Southern dent lines introduced to the United States from Mexico and Northern flints (NF) which were the predominant germplasm in the pre-Columbian era grown by the Native Americans<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 5" title="Anderson, E. &amp; Brown, W. L. The History of the Common Maize Varieties of the United States Corn Belt (Univ. of California Press, 1952)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0671-9#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d28078e637">5</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Doebley, J. et al. The origin of cornbelt maize: the isozyme evidence. Econ. Bot. 42, 120‚Äì131 (1988)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0671-9#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d28078e640">6</a></sup>. Historical records and genetic and molecular data strongly indicate that NFs originated from Native American populations of Southwestern America and the Great Plains<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2" title="Liu, K. et al. Genetic structure and diversity among maize inbred lines as inferred from DNA microsatellites. Genetics 165, 2117‚Äì2128 (2003)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0671-9#ref-CR2" id="ref-link-section-d28078e645">2</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Doebley, J. et al. The origin of cornbelt maize: the isozyme evidence. Econ. Bot. 42, 120‚Äì131 (1988)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0671-9#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d28078e648">6</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 7" title="van Heerwaarden, J., Hufford, M. B. &amp; Ross-Ibarra, J. Historical genomics of North American maize. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 109, 12420‚Äì12425 (2012)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0671-9#ref-CR7" id="ref-link-section-d28078e651">7</a></sup>. Improvement by early farmers and adaptation to cooler climate and different photoperiods extended NF growth as far north as Southern Canada. After the colonization of the New World, maize was spread to Europe both from the Caribbean islands and Northeast United States<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 8" title="Tenaillon, M. I. &amp; Charcosset, A. A European perspective on maize history. C. R. Biol. 334, 221‚Äì228 (2011)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0671-9#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d28078e655">8</a></sup>. The early maturing and cold-tolerant flints were key to a successful maize cultivation in temperate regions of Europe. Consequently, NF germplasm still makes a major contribution to modern European maize breeding material while Corn Belt dent genomes contain on average one-quarter from their blending with NFs<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2" title="Liu, K. et al. Genetic structure and diversity among maize inbred lines as inferred from DNA microsatellites. Genetics 165, 2117‚Äì2128 (2003)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0671-9#ref-CR2" id="ref-link-section-d28078e659">2</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Doebley, J. et al. The origin of cornbelt maize: the isozyme evidence. Econ. Bot. 42, 120‚Äì131 (1988)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0671-9#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d28078e662">6</a></sup>.</p><p>The complete genome sequence of the dent line B73 and its continuous updates provide a high-quality reference for maize breeding, genetic and genomic research<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 9" title="Jiao, Y. et al. Improved maize reference genome with single-molecule technologies. Nature 546, 524 (2017)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0671-9#ref-CR9" id="ref-link-section-d28078e669">9</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 10" title="Schnable, P. S. et al. The B73 maize genome: complexity, diversity, and dynamics. Science 326, 1112‚Äì1115 (2009)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0671-9#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d28078e672">10</a></sup>. However, very high diversity at both the sequence and genic level has been reported between maize inbred lines for targeted regions and large-scale comparisons<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 3" title="Chia, J. M. et al. Maize HapMap2 identifies extant variation from a genome in flux. Nat. Genet 44, 803‚Äì807 (2012)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0671-9#ref-CR3" id="ref-link-section-d28078e676">3</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Lai, J. et al. Gene loss and movement in the maize genome. Genome Res. 14, 1924‚Äì1931 (2004)." href="#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d28078e679">11</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Springer, N. M. et al. Maize inbreds exhibit high levels of copy number variation (CNV) and presence/absence variation (PAV) in genome content. PLoS Genet. 5, e1000734 (2009)." href="#ref-CR12" id="ref-link-section-d28078e679_1">12</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Swanson-Wagner, R. A. et al. Pervasive gene content variation and copy number variation in maize and its undomesticated progenitor. Genome Res. 20, 1689‚Äì1699 (2010)." href="#ref-CR13" id="ref-link-section-d28078e679_2">13</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 14" title="Wang, Q. &amp; Dooner, H. K. Remarkable variation in maize genome structure inferred from haplotype diversity at the bz locus. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 103, 17644‚Äì17649 (2006)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0671-9#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d28078e682">14</a></sup>. Hence, the B73 reference sequence captures only a portion of the maize pangenome. To overcome these limitations, a draft genome of the Iodent line PH207 (ref. <sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 15" title="Hirsch, C. N. et al. Draft assembly of elite inbred line PH207 provides insights into genomic and transcriptome diversity in maize. Plant Cell 28, 2700‚Äì2714 (2016)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0671-9#ref-CR15" id="ref-link-section-d28078e686">15</a></sup>) and‚Äîmore recently‚Äîreference sequences of SK, Mo17 and W22, a tropical and two US dent lines, have been released<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Springer, N. M. et al. The maize W22 genome provides a foundation for functional genomics and transposon biology. Nat. Genet. 50, 1282‚Äì1288 (2018)." href="#ref-CR16" id="ref-link-section-d28078e690">16</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Sun, S. et al. Extensive intraspecific gene order and gene structural variations between Mo17 and other maize genomes. Nat. Genet. 50, 1289‚Äì1295 (2018)." href="#ref-CR17" id="ref-link-section-d28078e690_1">17</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 18" title="Yang, N. et al. Genome assembly of a tropical maize inbred line provides insights into structural variation and crop improvement. Nat. Genet. 51, 1052‚Äì1059 (2019)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0671-9#ref-CR18" id="ref-link-section-d28078e693">18</a></sup>. Worldwide, many hybrid breeding programs focus on dent germplasm, whereas breeding programs in cooler regions of Central Europe exploit heterotic effects between dent and flint lines. Several studies have shown a clear differentiation of the North American and Northern European flint germplasm from the rest of the world<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 19" title="Unterseer, S. et al. A comprehensive study of the genomic differentiation between temperate Dent and Flint maize. Genome Biol. 17, 137 (2016)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0671-9#ref-CR19" id="ref-link-section-d28078e697">19</a></sup>. While reference-quality sequences exist for several dent inbred lines, the flint pool is still underexploited. To date, only one fragmented draft assembly with an N50 ~13.9‚Äâkilobases (kb) of one flint line, F2, is available covering ~65% of the estimated genome size<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 20" title="Darracq, A. et al. Sequence analysis of European maize inbred line F2 provides new insights into molecular and chromosomal characteristics of presence/absence variants. BMC Genomics 19, 119 (2018)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0671-9#ref-CR20" id="ref-link-section-d28078e702">20</a></sup>. To better understand the extent and quality of genomic differences between the flint and dent germplasm pools we generated de novo high-quality sequences for four flint inbred lines representing important ancestors of maize hybrid breeding in Central Europe<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 21" title="Messmer, M. M. et al. RFLP analyses of early-maturing European maize germ plasm: I. Genetic diversity among flint and dent inbreds. Theor. Appl. Genet. 83, 1003‚Äì1012 (1992)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0671-9#ref-CR21" id="ref-link-section-d28078e706">21</a></sup> and contrasted in this study their gene and repeat contents to two US Corn Belt dents.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec2" data-title="Results"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec2-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec2">Results</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec2-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec3">Whole-genome sequencing</h3><p>To access the genomic landscape of European flint maize, we assembled four flint lines to reference genome quality. Three lines are important founders of European breeding programs and represent distinct flint germplasm sources such as populations Lacaune (F7, Southern France), Lizargarate (EP1, Northern Spain) and Gelber Badischer Landmais (DK105, Southern Germany). The fourth sample, PE0075, is a doubled-haploid line derived from the Petkuser Ferdinand Rot population, a landrace from Northern Germany. We generated Illumina paired-end and mate-pair sequences equivalent to ~220‚Äì320√ó coverage (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-0671-9#MOESM1">1</a>) and assembled the reads to scaffolds and pseudomolecules using the DeNovoMagic pipeline<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 15" title="Hirsch, C. N. et al. Draft assembly of elite inbred line PH207 provides insights into genomic and transcriptome diversity in maize. Plant Cell 28, 2700‚Äì2714 (2016)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0671-9#ref-CR15" id="ref-link-section-d28078e725">15</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 16" title="Springer, N. M. et al. The maize W22 genome provides a foundation for functional genomics and transposon biology. Nat. Genet. 50, 1282‚Äì1288 (2018)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0671-9#ref-CR16" id="ref-link-section-d28078e728">16</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 22" title="International Wheat Genome Sequencing Consortium et al. Shifting the limits in wheat research and breeding using a fully annotated reference genome. Science 361, 7191 (2018)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0671-9#ref-CR22" id="ref-link-section-d28078e731">22</a></sup>. Total assembly sizes of the pseudochromosomes range from 2.14 to 2.32‚Äâmegabases (Mb) and amount to 92.9‚Äì100.5% of the genome sizes estimated by <i>K</i>-mer analysis (Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-0671-9#Tab1">1</a> and Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-0671-9#MOESM1">1</a>). Most (94.3‚Äì97.3%) contig and scaffold sequences were integrated into ten pseudochromosomes (Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-0671-9#Tab1">1</a>, Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-0671-9#MOESM1">2</a> and Extended Data Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-0671-9#Fig6">1a</a>). A genetic map generated from an F<sub>2</sub> mapping population of an EP1‚Äâ√ó‚ÄâPH207 cross demonstrated a high consensus between genetic and physical map corroborating quality and contiguity of the maize assemblies (Extended Data Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-0671-9#Fig7">2</a>). Sequence accuracy was high with less than one erroneous base per 100‚Äâkb (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-0671-9#MOESM1">3</a>) and remapping genomic reads using SQUAT<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 23" title="Yang, L. A. et al. SQUAT: a Sequencing Quality Assessment Tool for data quality assessments of genome assemblies. BMC Genomics 19, 238 (2019)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0671-9#ref-CR23" id="ref-link-section-d28078e763">23</a></sup> reported &lt;2% poorly mapped reads. Complete BUSCO<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 24" title="Sim√£o, F. A. et al. BUSCO: assessing genome assembly and annotation completeness with single-copy orthologs. Bioinformatics 31, 3210‚Äì3212 (2015)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0671-9#ref-CR24" id="ref-link-section-d28078e767">24</a></sup> genes totaled &gt;95%, strongly supporting high coverage of the gene space while only ~1.5% and 0.8‚Äì2.4% of the BUSCO Liliopsidae set were absent or fragmented in each of the four assemblies on average, respectively (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-0671-9#MOESM1">2</a>). In addition, the long terminal repeat (LTR) assembly index<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 25" title="Ou, S., Chen, J. &amp; Jiang, N. Assessing genome assembly quality using the LTR Assembly Index (LAI). Nucleic Acids Res. 46, e126 (2018)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0671-9#ref-CR25" id="ref-link-section-d28078e774">25</a></sup>, a measure for the correct reconstruction of the repetitive transposon space, supports the reference quality of our four flint assemblies (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-0671-9#MOESM1">4</a>).</p><div class="c-article-table" data-test="inline-table" data-container-section="table" id="table-1"><figure><figcaption class="c-article-table__figcaption"><b id="Tab1" data-test="table-caption">Table 1 Genome statistics of maize dent and flint lines used in this study</b></figcaption><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="table-link" data-track="click" data-track-action="view table" data-track-label="button" rel="nofollow" href="/articles/s41588-020-0671-9/tables/1"><span>Full size table</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><h4 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--small" id="Sec4">Pan-gene variation among flint and dent is moderate</h4><p>To assess genic presence‚Äìabsence variations (PAVs) in the six maize lines we used a three-layer gene prediction pipeline. For F7 and EP1 we predicted protein-coding genes as consensus models using protein homologies of known monocotyledonous protein sequences and a broad spectrum of transcriptome evidences from F7 and EP1 RNA-seq data (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-0671-9#MOESM1">5</a>). Subsequently, cross-mapping of the EP1 and F7 models as well as annotations of B73 (v.4) and PH207 (v.1.1) complemented the gene sets of all six maize lines including PE0075 and DK105. In total, we identified ~46,200‚Äì48,000 consolidated gene models per line (Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-0671-9#Tab1">1</a>). Cross-mapping and consolidation substantially improved the number of pairwise reciprocal best blast orthologs (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-0671-9#Fig1">1a</a> and Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-0671-9#MOESM1">6</a>) as well as the completeness of orthologous clusters (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-0671-9#Fig1">1b</a> and Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-0671-9#MOESM1">2</a>).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-1" data-title="Gene consolidation and characteristics."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig1" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 1: Gene consolidation and characteristics.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41588-020-0671-9/figures/1" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41588-020-0671-9/MediaObjects/41588_2020_671_Fig1_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-1-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41588-020-0671-9/MediaObjects/41588_2020_671_Fig1_HTML.png" alt="figure1" loading="lazy" width="685" height="513" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-1-desc"><p><b>a</b>, Comparison of reciprocal best blast orthologs (bbh), genes with a TPM‚Äâ‚â•0.1 (exp) and angiosperm proteins (hom) between the consolidated (B73c and PH207c) and initial B73 and PH207 gene models. <b>b</b>, The number of genes per line that are either singletons (label 1) or syntenic between two and six lines (labels 2‚Äì6). <b>c</b>, Expression levels (TPM) of EP1 and F7 genes graded by size of their orthologous clusters. Genes either are singletons (cluster size 1) or have orthologs in one to five maize lines (cluster sizes 2‚Äì6). Syntelogs of orthologous clusters comprising 2‚Äì4 lines show very low median expression levels (TPM‚Äâ~0.06). <b>d</b>, Illustration per cluster size of the proportion of orthologous groups with support by bbh and homology (purple), only bbh (red), only homology (hom, blue) and residual clusters (res, tan) matching neither the homology nor the bbh criterion. To record homology, a minimum alignment length of 80% of both maize and angiosperm protein was required in at least three species. The high conservation observed in cluster size 1 reflects, in part, enrichment of tandemly repeated genes duplicated from orthologs of clusters present in five or six lines.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41588-020-0671-9/figures/1" data-track-dest="link:Figure1 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>Overall, 43,700‚Äì45,900 genes (94.7‚Äì98% of all genes per line) have an orthologous counterpart or reciprocal best blast match in at least one of the other lines (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-0671-9#Fig1">1b</a>). Comparison of the original and consolidated annotations of B73 and PH207 suggests that part of this gene complement displays functional signatures such as homology to angiosperm proteins or expression in the respective line (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-0671-9#Fig1">1a</a> and Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-0671-9#MOESM1">3</a>). The cross-mapped annotation significantly improved the completeness of syntelog clusters with 34,352 clusters that have syntelogs for all six lines, and 94.6% of these contain exactly one gene copy per line while 1,861 (5.4%) contain additional co-orthologous members (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-0671-9#Fig1">1b</a>). Genic PAVs strongly support flint and dent as distinctive germplasm (Extended Data Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-0671-9#Fig8">3</a>). Overall, core genes present in all six maize lines show high expression levels (mean and median 25.8 and 5.9‚Äâtranscripts per million, TPM) while PAV genes show significantly lower expression levels (median 0.02‚Äì0.06‚ÄâTPM) and have lower homology support in a comparison to 27 angiosperms (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-0671-9#Fig1">1c,d</a>). Note that genes found in only one line in general show more pronounced support for protein-coding sequences compared to genes of cluster sizes 2‚Äì4. Genes with no corresponding maize ortholog (cluster size 1) are enriched for tandem duplications (Fisher exact test with <i>P</i> values between &lt;1‚Äâ√ó‚Äâ10<sup>‚àí8</sup> and 1‚Äâ√ó‚Äâ10<sup>‚àí70</sup>). The very low median expression of syntenic dispensable and singleton genes suggests no, only minor or highly specialized line-specific functions for most of these genes.</p><p>Consistent with the genome analysis of the maize inbred line Mo17 (ref. <sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 17" title="Sun, S. et al. Extensive intraspecific gene order and gene structural variations between Mo17 and other maize genomes. Nat. Genet. 50, 1289‚Äì1295 (2018)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0671-9#ref-CR17" id="ref-link-section-d28078e1038">17</a></sup>), our cross-consolidation revealed a sizeable number of predicted genes with large effect mutations. Between 2,487 (B73) and up to 4,264 (DK105) gene models had no contiguous ORF due to internal stop codons or frameshifts. Genes with disrupted ORFs had highly similar expression levels (Pearson correlation 0.93‚Äâ&lt;‚Äâ<i>r</i>‚Äâ&lt;‚Äâ0.95) to their syntenic partner with contiguous reading frame (Extended Data Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-0671-9#Fig9">4</a>). Close inspection revealed a series of reasons including pseudogenization, missing splice variants or skipped exons in the respective line.</p><h4 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--small" id="Sec5">Dynamics of the full-length retrotransposon landscape</h4><p>Approximately 80% of the maize genome is constituted by repeats of different types. In addition to the six lines above, the recently published genomes Mo17 and W22 were analyzed for their repeat content<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 16" title="Springer, N. M. et al. The maize W22 genome provides a foundation for functional genomics and transposon biology. Nat. Genet. 50, 1282‚Äì1288 (2018)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0671-9#ref-CR16" id="ref-link-section-d28078e1056">16</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 17" title="Sun, S. et al. Extensive intraspecific gene order and gene structural variations between Mo17 and other maize genomes. Nat. Genet. 50, 1289‚Äì1295 (2018)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0671-9#ref-CR17" id="ref-link-section-d28078e1059">17</a></sup>. No pronounced differences in transposable element (TE) composition among the eight lines analyzed was detected (Extended Data Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-0671-9#Fig6">1</a> and Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-0671-9#MOESM1">7</a>). We detected almost 15,000 high-quality full-length LTRs (fl-LTRs) per line, matching the expected genome size to fl-LTR ratio<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 22" title="International Wheat Genome Sequencing Consortium et al. Shifting the limits in wheat research and breeding using a fully annotated reference genome. Science 361, 7191 (2018)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0671-9#ref-CR22" id="ref-link-section-d28078e1069">22</a></sup> (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-0671-9#MOESM1">8a</a>). The lower number observed for PH207 (6,838 fl-LTRs) can be attributed the overall lower quality assembly compared to the other five lines. Correspondingly, the PH207 assembly was excluded from evaluation depending on the analysis criteria. For the remaining five lines, the number and age distribution of fl-LTRs confirm the high quality of the respective assemblies (Extended Data Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-0671-9#Fig10">5</a>).</p><p>To quantify transposon dynamics between the different maize lines we identified still-shared syntenic fl-LTRs by clustering TE junctions with high stringency. Only 3% of all fl-LTR locations were found to be shared between six lines. While the percentage of shared elements among the different lines shows a marked decrease, the pangenome of the fl-LTR space almost doubles the number of elements accordingly. This high dynamic is in stark contrast to genes where 76.1% are retained at their syntenic position (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-0671-9#Fig2">2a</a>). Pairwise cross-comparison among the different genomes detected between 18 and 32% of still syntenic fl-LTRs to be present in the corresponding line. Differences in pairwise shared numbers match the phylogenies from gene-derived phylogenetic relationships and reveal a clear distinction between flint and dent lines (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-0671-9#Fig2">2b</a>). Line-specific fl-LTRs are younger and depleted in the pericentromeric regions. Along with increased sharing, a continuous enrichment towards the central low recombining compartments is observed and associated with an increase in age (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-0671-9#Fig2">2c</a>). Our findings illustrate the rapid turnover of the intergenic space most likely driven by elimination through illegitimate recombination.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-2" data-title="Pan and core characteristics of fl-LTR."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig2" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 2: Pan and core characteristics of fl-LTR.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41588-020-0671-9/figures/2" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41588-020-0671-9/MediaObjects/41588_2020_671_Fig2_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-2-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41588-020-0671-9/MediaObjects/41588_2020_671_Fig2_HTML.png" alt="figure2" loading="lazy" width="685" height="596" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-2-desc"><p><b>a</b>, Proportion of the pan and core sets for fl-LTRs and genes with increasing line numbers. <b>b</b>, Percentage of pairwise shared and still-intact fl-LTRs at syntenic positions for seven maize lines. Reading direction is column to row; for example, EP1 shares 27% of its fl-LTRs with F7 and F7 shares 28% with EP1. On the basis of the similarity matrix, the seven lines cluster into a relationship context that separates flint and dent. Within flint and dent, around 30% of the locations are shared; between flint and dent, most of the values are reduced to about 20%, except for PE0075 (25%). The most pronounced overlap of intact and shared fl-LTR locations is found between W22 and B73 (32%); the least pronounced is found between EP1 and Mo17 (18%). A corresponding evalution for genes gives pairwise shared numbers between 82 and 91%. <b>c</b>, Insertion age (<i>y</i> axis) and chromosomal distribution of all fl-LTRs (top row), line unique (label 1) and cluster constellations of increased sharing ranging from two to all eight lines. The chromosomal location is collapsed for all ten chromosomes and given in percentage of the respective chromosome length. The line-specific or shared among fewer lines fl-LTRs contain a higher proportion of younger elements and are less frequently found in the central, low recombining regions. There is a continuous shift towards a more pericentromeric location and towards older elements with the increase of lines sharing corresponding elements.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41588-020-0671-9/figures/2" data-track-dest="link:Figure2 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><h4 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--small" id="Sec6">Heterochromatic knob islands in the maize genome</h4><p>Knob regions are heterochromatic regions in the genome that have been demonstrated to affect local recombination<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 26" title="Ghaffari, R. et al. Maize chromosomal knobs are located in gene-dense areas and suppress local recombination. Chromosoma 122, 67‚Äì75 (2013)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0671-9#ref-CR26" id="ref-link-section-d28078e1120">26</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 27" title="Stack, S. M. et al. Meiotic crossing over in maize knob heterochromatin. Genetics 205, 1101‚Äì1112 (2017)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0671-9#ref-CR27" id="ref-link-section-d28078e1123">27</a></sup>. They belong to the group of satellite tandem repeats which are well represented in our flint assemblies (Extended Data Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-0671-9#Fig11">6a</a>, Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-0671-9#MOESM1">4</a> and Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-0671-9#MOESM1">8b</a>). Maize knobs are composed of 180‚Äâbp and closely related 202-bp tandemly repeated sequence units (Extended Data Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-0671-9#Fig11">6c</a>). Variations in position and extent of knob regions in maize have been documented<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 26" title="Ghaffari, R. et al. Maize chromosomal knobs are located in gene-dense areas and suppress local recombination. Chromosoma 122, 67‚Äì75 (2013)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0671-9#ref-CR26" id="ref-link-section-d28078e1140">26</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 28" title="Ananiev, E. V., Phillips, R. L. &amp; Rines, H. W. Complex structure of knobs and centromeric regions in maize chromosomes. Tsitol. Genet. 34, 11‚Äì15 (2000)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0671-9#ref-CR28" id="ref-link-section-d28078e1143">28</a></sup>. Intensity and position of knob regions detected by FISH for the different karyotypes clearly separate dent from flint lines (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-0671-9#Fig3">3a</a> and Extended Data Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-0671-9#Fig11">6b</a>). In particular, extensive knob regions detected on chromosomes 7 and 8 of the dent lines are absent in flint lines. For other positions on the genome, a clear flint/dent separation among lines cannot be observed. However, the observed variations reveal the pervasive dynamics of knob regions in the genome. FISH requires a minimum number of adjacent tandem units for their detection. High-quality genome assemblies resolving a larger proportion of the repetitive space allow analyzing the so-far-hidden, cryptic, knob sites which we annotated by homology to the 180 and 202‚Äâbp monomers. Besides the large knob regions detected by FISH, numerous additional positions with fewer tandem repeat units are found in the sequence assembly on all chromosomes (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-0671-9#Fig3">3b</a> and Extended Data Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-0671-9#Fig12">7</a>). A strikingly pronounced syntenic conservation of minor knob sites common to all analyzed flint and dent lines was observed. Given the observations of rapid diversification based on repeat units detailed above, this might indicate numerous potential (shadowed) knob regions that can rapidly expand or shrink by, for example, illegitimate recombination and can cause recombinational isolation of the affected regions.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-3" data-title="Diversity of knob locations in six maize lines."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig3" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 3: Diversity of knob locations in six maize lines.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41588-020-0671-9/figures/3" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41588-020-0671-9/MediaObjects/41588_2020_671_Fig3_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-3-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41588-020-0671-9/MediaObjects/41588_2020_671_Fig3_HTML.png" alt="figure3" loading="lazy" width="685" height="702" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-3-desc"><p><b>a</b>, Karyotyping of all six maize lines by FISH. Colors show: red, (ACT)10; green, Knob-2 (180-bp knob repeat) and MR68-3 (chromosome 6 clone MR68, ID <a href="https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/nuccore/AF020265.1">AF020265.1</a>); magenta, CentC69-1. Green arrows indicate co-occurrences of Knob-2 and MR68-3; orange arrows indicate a sole occurrence of MR68-3. <b>b</b>, Chromosomal locations of all knob sequences in EP1. Besides the three major Mb scaled knob regions identified by FISH on chromosome 4L, 5L and 6S, many smaller knob sequences (&lt;10‚Äâkb; below the FISH detection limit) are found scattered along all chromosomes. Interestingly, many of the minor knob locations are shared between the lines (Extended Data Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-0671-9#Fig12">7</a>). The polymorphisms seen by FISH seem to be based on large size variations of the mostly syntenic minor sites (7). <b>c</b>, Relation between gene neighborhood and gene expression. For each gene the maximal expression of seven different conditions is plotted against its upstream distance (bp) to the next neighboring element of a specific type. Both axes are logarithmic. It is noteworthy that knob sequences in particular seem to be a ‚Äòbad‚Äô neighborhood for genes. Here, in contrast to other elements a 1,000-bp perimeter was found to be devoid of genes.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41588-020-0671-9/figures/3" data-track-dest="link:Figure3 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>In fission yeast, knob structures have been shown to implicate significant downregulation of surrounding genes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 29" title="Matsuda, A. et al. Highly condensed chromatins are formed adjacent to subtelomeric and decondensed silent chromatin in fission yeast. Nat. Commun. 6, 7753 (2015)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0671-9#ref-CR29" id="ref-link-section-d28078e1186">29</a></sup>. To check for similar effects in maize we analyzed the distance of genes to the nearest knob signature in the assembly and related it to expression data<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 30" title="Kremling, K. A. G. et al. Dysregulation of expression correlates with rare-allele burden and fitness loss in maize. Nature 555, 520 (2018)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0671-9#ref-CR30" id="ref-link-section-d28078e1190">30</a></sup> generated for the lines B73, EP1 and F7. Even more pronounced than observed for other types of repeat elements, we find the close surrounding of knob repeat units to be devoid of genes (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-0671-9#Fig3">3c</a>). For genes surrounding knob regions, we observe a trend for lower expression values (Extended Data Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-0671-9#Fig12">7d</a>).</p><h4 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--small" id="Sec7">Whole-genome alignment</h4><p>Numerous large structural variations (SV) in maize have been reported<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 11" title="Lai, J. et al. Gene loss and movement in the maize genome. Genome Res. 14, 1924‚Äì1931 (2004)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0671-9#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d28078e1209">11</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 12" title="Springer, N. M. et al. Maize inbreds exhibit high levels of copy number variation (CNV) and presence/absence variation (PAV) in genome content. PLoS Genet. 5, e1000734 (2009)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0671-9#ref-CR12" id="ref-link-section-d28078e1212">12</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 14" title="Wang, Q. &amp; Dooner, H. K. Remarkable variation in maize genome structure inferred from haplotype diversity at the bz locus. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 103, 17644‚Äì17649 (2006)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0671-9#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d28078e1215">14</a></sup>. To gain insight into the extent of SVs in the maize flint versus dent genomes we generated pairwise whole-genome alignments (WGAs) for the six lines and postprocessed the resulting alignments to single alignment blocks (SAB) that represent the highest scoring one-to-one relationships between each genome pair. To approximate overall contiguity between two genomes, SABs were further concatenated to merged alignment blocks (MABs) if they followed a strict and unambiguous order in both genome sequences (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-0671-9#MOESM1">5</a>). On average, ~50% of the genome sequence aligned in each of the pairwise comparisons by SAB scoring, while WGAs (MABs) associated 80‚Äì90% (1.7‚Äì2‚ÄâGb) of the genome sequences (Supplementary Tables <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-0671-9#MOESM1">9</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-0671-9#MOESM1">10</a>). Mean sizes of SABs and MABs were ~10‚Äâkb and ~100‚Äâkb, respectively (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-0671-9#MOESM1">6</a>). Evaluation of pairwise WGA regions strongly supported orthology of segments in parallel orientations (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-0671-9#MOESM1">7</a>) while an assessment of most rearrangements (inversions and transpositions) was inconclusive due to insufficient or ambiguous information at the breakpoints.</p><p>To delineate regions that do not align with any other of the five lines (unaligned), align solely with flint or dent maize lines (group-specific) or are aligned in all pairwise alignments (core), we combined pairwise WGAs of each line and projected them onto its genome sequence (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-0671-9#MOESM1">8</a>). We classified a genomic region as core type if it aligned to four out of the five possible lines due to the high amount of gap sequences in PH207 that likely lead to an underestimation of the true core genome. The core genome defined by SABs comprised on average ~850‚ÄâMb (40%) of the total genome while 71‚ÄâMb (3.3%) were group-specific and ~460‚ÄâMb (22%) were unaligned. This indicates a large fraction of uniquely inserted or deleted sequences in each line. Positional analysis of genomic bins revealed significant positive correlations (Kendall‚Äôs <i>œÑ</i>‚Äâ‚â•‚Äâ0.38; <i>P</i>‚Äâ&lt;‚Äâ10<sup>‚Äì36</sup>) between the core and repeat density while gene densities were negatively correlated (‚àí0.4‚Äâ&lt;‚Äâ<i>œÑ</i>‚Äâ&lt;‚Äâ‚àí0.33; <i>P</i>‚Äâ&lt;‚Äâ10<sup>‚Äì26</sup>) (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-0671-9#Fig4">4a</a> and Extended Data Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-0671-9#Fig13">8</a>). Notably, for all six lines highest core densities located at regions adjacent to the centromere and correspond to above-average SAB sizes and below-average recombination rates (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-0671-9#Fig4">4b</a>). These findings are reminiscent of observations in the Triticeae gene space<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 22" title="International Wheat Genome Sequencing Consortium et al. Shifting the limits in wheat research and breeding using a fully annotated reference genome. Science 361, 7191 (2018)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0671-9#ref-CR22" id="ref-link-section-d28078e1267">22</a></sup> mirroring the recombinogenic properties of centromeric and telomeric regions.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-4" data-title="Comparative genome characteristics for the six maize lines."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig4" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 4: Comparative genome characteristics for the six maize lines.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41588-020-0671-9/figures/4" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41588-020-0671-9/MediaObjects/41588_2020_671_Fig4_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-4-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41588-020-0671-9/MediaObjects/41588_2020_671_Fig4_HTML.png" alt="figure4" loading="lazy" width="685" height="508" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-4-desc"><p><b>a</b>, The density distribution of the WGA (co, core; sp, germplasm-specific; un, unaligned regions) and functional genomic elements (re, all repeats; gy and cp, gypsy and copia LTR elements; ge, genes). The core WGAs are significantly positively correlated to the repeat and gypsy densities, similar to densities of copia elements and genes. For the unaligned and germplasm-specific densities, we detected no highly significant correlations with one of the other functional elements. <b>b</b>, Densities of the core, germplasm-specific and unaligned WGA regions showed similar distributions for all maize lines, exemplified for chromosome 1 of B73, PH207, EP1, F7 and PE0075. Core regions are enriched at (peri-)centromeric regions, germplasm-specific obviously cluster in a group-specific manner and unaligned parts are random within all six lines. <b>c</b>, Runs-of-identity for SNPs from core regions for chromosome 7 and all 15 pairwise combinations. Color code above the chromosome pictograms follows legend in <b>d</b>. <b>d</b>, Sizes of higher order haplotypes of the six lines. Pictograms are proportional to their total genomic span, the legend provides the color coding of each of the six lines. Black lines indicate identity. Interestingly, the top six higher order haplotypes each group one maize variety as outlier and five lines that are identical by their SNP runs for these genomic segments. Of the more complex groupings, the most prominent haplotype separates the two germplasm, flint and dent.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41588-020-0671-9/figures/4" data-track-dest="link:Figure4 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><h4 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--small" id="Sec8">Genetic mosaicism in US dents and European flints</h4><p>The core genomic regions served as positional anchors to generate multiple sequence alignments. One-to-one unambiguous alignments for the six lines total 287‚ÄâMb (~15%) of each genome and comprise 6.25‚Äâ√ó‚Äâ10<sup>6</sup> orthologous SNPs with positional information for each of the six genomic coordinate systems. The detected SNPs showed an excellent agreement of 99.3% (4,827 mismatches out of 653,398 total scored calls) to a published set of SNP calls using the Affymetrix Axiom Maize Genotyping Array with 600,000 variants<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 31" title="Unterseer, S. et al. A powerful tool for genome analysis in maize: development and evaluation of the high density 600 k SNP genotyping array. BMC Genomics 15, 823 (2014)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0671-9#ref-CR31" id="ref-link-section-d28078e1305">31</a></sup>. On the basis of sequences of identical SNP pairs, we determined significant haplotypes between all 15 line combinations (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-0671-9#Fig4">4c</a>). Mean and total genomic spans covered by these pairwise near-identical haplotypes range from 40 to 68‚Äâkb and 394 to 664‚ÄâMb, respectively. The proportion of one-fifth to one-third shared genomic regions between flint and dent lines is in close agreement with previous studies of NF content in stiff stalk and nonstiff stalk dents<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2" title="Liu, K. et al. Genetic structure and diversity among maize inbred lines as inferred from DNA microsatellites. Genetics 165, 2117‚Äì2128 (2003)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0671-9#ref-CR2" id="ref-link-section-d28078e1312">2</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Doebley, J. et al. The origin of cornbelt maize: the isozyme evidence. Econ. Bot. 42, 120‚Äì131 (1988)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0671-9#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d28078e1315">6</a></sup>. Number, mean and total sizes were highly consistent with their phylogenetic relationship deduced from high-confidence orthologs (Extended Data Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-0671-9#Fig14">9</a>). However, each line of one group (flint or dent) shared substantial genomic portions with the other germplasm group (394 to 480‚ÄâMb for B73 and EP1 or PE0075, respectively). This is in line with the history of US Corn Belt dents that originated from crosses of Southern dents and flints of North America and Canada<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Doebley, J. et al. The origin of cornbelt maize: the isozyme evidence. Econ. Bot. 42, 120‚Äì131 (1988)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0671-9#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d28078e1323">6</a></sup>. The latter established many founder lines of modern European flint breeding and were introduced to Europe as early as the discovery of the New World<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 8" title="Tenaillon, M. I. &amp; Charcosset, A. A European perspective on maize history. C. R. Biol. 334, 221‚Äì228 (2011)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0671-9#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d28078e1327">8</a></sup>. Alternatively, these haplotypes could be introgressions of US germplasm into European flint material that started in the 1950s to broaden genetic diversity in European breeding programs. Notably, the DH line of the European landrace Petkuser showed the largest genome-wide similarity of all flints towards B73.</p><p>Next, we surveyed higher order haplotypes considering all 15 pairwise similarities simultaneously as combined binary patterns. Limiting these haplotypes to a minimum run size of 40 SNPs, we identified 31 fitting this size criterion (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-0671-9#Fig4">4d</a> and Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-0671-9#MOESM1">11</a>). Since the identified regions exceed random expectation by far and show near-complete SNP identity across particular line subsets/combinations, these regions are likely identical-by-descent and share a common ancestor. In total, the 31 distinct higher order haplotypes span ~288‚ÄâMb and comprise &gt;1.1‚Äâ√ó‚Äâ10<sup>6</sup> orthologous SNPs. Note that ordering by the number of SNPs as well as the genomic region covered, the top five/six haplotypes encode combinatorial groupings with five out of six lines being identical. The haplotype distinguishing between the studied dent and European flint lines ranked only as the sixth (by size) or seventh (by SNP number) most frequent (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-0671-9#Fig4">4d</a> and Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-0671-9#MOESM1">11</a>). The distinct higher order haplotypes distribute evenly along the ten chromosomes but also comprised several striking clusters. Although not the most prominent identical-by-descent type, three regions were enriched for the haplotype distinctive between flint and dent lines: two at the distal sites of chromosomes 4 and 7 and a large portion spanning ~12‚ÄâMb in the proximal part of chromosome 8. The latter strongly coincides with group-specific WGAs on chromosome 8 (Extended Data Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-0671-9#Fig13">8</a>) and encloses the major flowering time quantititive trait locus <i>vgt1</i> in maize<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 32" title="Salvi, S. et al. Conserved noncoding genomic sequences associated with a flowering-time quantitative trait locus in maize. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 104, 11376‚Äì11381 (2007)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0671-9#ref-CR32" id="ref-link-section-d28078e1355">32</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 33" title="Ducrocq, S. et al. Key impact of Vgt1 on flowering time adaptation in maize: evidence from association mapping and ecogeographical information. Genetics 178, 2433‚Äì2437 (2008)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0671-9#ref-CR33" id="ref-link-section-d28078e1358">33</a></sup>.</p><h4 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--small" id="Sec9">Haplotype-informed differential gene expression analysis</h4><p>To explore putative functional consequences of the haplotype differentiating our dent and European flint lines, we analyzed its effect on the maize transcriptome. From the maize association panel we selected a subset of 40 out of 282 lines, which were highly similar to either B73 or F7/EP1 within regions defined by this haplotype (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-0671-9#MOESM1">9</a>)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 34" title="Bukowski, R. et al. Construction of the third-generation Zea mays haplotype map. Gigascience 7, 1‚Äì12 (2018)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0671-9#ref-CR34" id="ref-link-section-d28078e1373">34</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 35" title="Cook, J. P. et al. Genetic architecture of maize kernel composition in the nested association mapping and inbred association panels. Plant Physiol. 158, 824‚Äì834 (2012)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0671-9#ref-CR35" id="ref-link-section-d28078e1376">35</a></sup>. We analyzed genome-wide expression levels between the two contrasting groups underlying three different genome references (B73, EP1 and F7) and using expression data from seven different conditions and tissues<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 30" title="Kremling, K. A. G. et al. Dysregulation of expression correlates with rare-allele burden and fitness loss in maize. Nature 555, 520 (2018)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0671-9#ref-CR30" id="ref-link-section-d28078e1380">30</a></sup>. In total, 4,761 genes orthologous between B73, EP1 and F7 (out of 35,389 triplets) were differentially expressed (DEGs) in all three references and in at least one of the tissues and conditions (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-0671-9#Fig5">5</a>).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-5" data-title="Haploblock-informed DEGs."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig5" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 5: Haploblock-informed DEGs.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41588-020-0671-9/figures/5" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41588-020-0671-9/MediaObjects/41588_2020_671_Fig5_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-5-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41588-020-0671-9/MediaObjects/41588_2020_671_Fig5_HTML.png" alt="figure5" loading="lazy" width="685" height="505" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-5-desc"><p><b>a</b>, Heatmap of top 30 DEGs (log<sub>2</sub>-fold change above 2 or below ‚Äì2, adjusted <i>P</i>‚Äâ<i>‚â§</i>‚Äâ0.05, variance stabilized) between the two haploblock-informed groups in kernels. Group 1, lines with haploblock-similarity to B73 (dent-alike); group 2, lines with haploblock-similarity to EP1/F7 (flint-alike). <b>b</b>, DEGs between the two groups that encode for components of the starch biosynthesis pathway, for proteins involved in sugar metabolism and transport into the kernel, and for proteins organizing the prolamin matrix structure of the kernel. <i>sbe1</i>, starch branching enzyme 1; <i>agpsl1</i>, ADP-glucose pyrophosphorylase small subunit leaf1; <i>sh2</i>, <i>shrunken2</i>; <i>ss1</i>, starch synthase 1; <i>pho1</i>, starch phosphorylase 1; sweet, sugar transporter; <i>su1</i>: <i>sugary1</i>; <i>stp1</i>, sugar transport protein 1; hex, hexokinase; <i>o7</i>, <i>opaque7</i>; <i>nkd1</i>, <i>naked endosperm</i>; <i>fl3</i>, <i>floury endosperm 3</i>; <i>PWD</i>, phosphoglucan, water dikinase; <i>GWD1</i>, glucan, water dikinase 1. <b>c</b>, Main reactions of starch biosynthesis and catalyzing enzymes, genes differentially expressed between the two groups of maize lines highlighted in blue.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41588-020-0671-9/figures/5" data-track-dest="link:Figure5 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>Comparing identified DEGs with curated maize genes revealed a striking pattern for genes involved in the establishment of the kernel texture. We identified a plethora of genes involved in starch metabolism: sugar transporters including several proteins of the SWEET family, <i>hex4</i> and <i>hex7</i>, sugar transporter <i>stp1</i>, the rate-limiting gene for starch biosynthesis (<i>shrunken2</i>), <i>starch synthase1</i> (<i>ss1</i>), <i>starch phosphorylase</i>, the Œ±(1‚Äâ‚Üí‚Äâ6)-glucosylhydrolase <i>sugary</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 36" title="James, M. G., Robertson, D. S. &amp; Myers, A. M. Characterization of the maize gene sugary1, a determinant of starch composition in kernels. Plant Cell 7, 417‚Äì429 (1995)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0671-9#ref-CR36" id="ref-link-section-d28078e1493">36</a></sup> and starch branching enzyme <i>sbe1</i> catalyzing the formation of Œ±(1‚Äâ‚Üí‚Äâ6)-glycosidic bonds in amylopectin (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-0671-9#Fig5">5c</a>). We also detected a number of genes that establish and regulate the prolamin content and structure of the maize endosperm (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-0671-9#Fig5">5b</a>) including <i>opaque endosperm o7</i> and <i>o10</i>, <i>empty pericarp 16</i>, <i>floury endosperm fl3</i> and <i>naked endosperm nkd1</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Yang, J., Ji, C. &amp; Wu, Y. Divergent transactivation of maize storage protein zein genes by the transcription factors Opaque2 and OHPs. Genetics 204, 581‚Äì591 (2016)." href="#ref-CR37" id="ref-link-section-d28078e1522">37</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Yao, D. et al. Maize opaque10 encodes a cereal-specific protein that is essential for the proper distribution of zeins in endosperm protein bodies. PLoS Genet. 12, e1006270 (2016)." href="#ref-CR38" id="ref-link-section-d28078e1522_1">38</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Yi, G. et al. The naked endosperm genes encode duplicate INDETERMINATE domain transcription factors required for maize endosperm cell patterning and differentiation. Plant Physiol. 167, 443‚Äì456 (2015)." href="#ref-CR39" id="ref-link-section-d28078e1522_2">39</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 40" title="Zhang, S., Zhan, J. &amp; Yadegari, R. Maize opaque mutants are no longer so opaque. Plant Reprod. 31, 319‚Äì326 (2018)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0671-9#ref-CR40" id="ref-link-section-d28078e1525">40</a></sup>.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec10" data-title="Discussion"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec10-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec10">Discussion</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec10-content"><p>The comparative analysis of the gene and repeat content together with the analysis of WGAs and SNPs confirms the European flints as highly differentiated germplasm of cultivated maize at a whole-genome scale. For all these analyses, the four flint and the two dent lines form two distinct groups, with each line sharing larger numbers of syntenic genes, repeats, aligned regions and haplotypes with members of the same group than with any member of the other group. Nevertheless, we also observed significant overall conservation of chromosome structure and gene content. Aligned blocks ordered by consecutive syntenic order span ~85% of the entire genome in all lines. Cross-consolidation of gene models revealed a moderate number of nonsyntenic genes, suggesting that genic PAVs are less pronounced in maize than previously reported. Consistently, significant lower support by expression and homology indicates that less conserved orthologous clusters are likely enriched for nonfunctional and poor gene models. Nevertheless, the latter set also contains many genes expressed at moderate to high levels, which might contribute to line-specific adaptations and are of interest for maize improvement and breeding. Additionally, some of these genes may have highly confined expression patterns or conditions that were missed in our transcriptome analysis. The cross-consolidation approach also had several limitations including the potential transfer of poor gene models and the identification of genes that contain large effect mutations and disrupted ORFs. Inspection of the latter set revealed several causes including likely true pseudogenizations or truncations, probable differences in the exon‚Äìintron structure between the applied informant and target gene model as well as mapping errors.</p><p>In contrast to the overall syntenic conservation, a large amount of unaligned, nonorthologous sequences for WGAs and the analysis of the repeat space demonstrated enormous diversity and dynamics of the maize genome. Only 2% of shared orthologous fl-LTRs are conserved in eight lines.
