Consistent with previous reports, large FISH visible knobs display substantial variation in position among the maize lines studied<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 28" title="Ananiev, E. V., Phillips, R. L. &amp; Rines, H. W. Complex structure of knobs and centromeric regions in maize chromosomes. Tsitol. Genet. 34, 11‚Äì15 (2000)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0671-9#ref-CR28" id="ref-link-section-d28078e1542">28</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 41" title="Brown, W. L. Numbers and distribution of chromosome knobs in United States maize. Genetics 34, 524‚Äì536 (1949)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0671-9#ref-CR41" id="ref-link-section-d28078e1545">41</a></sup>. Remarkably, both shared sets of orthologous LTRs and larger knob positions perfectly reproduced the relationship of the six lines based on coding sequences, indicating smooth transitions between these features during breeding and admixture. In contrast to previous studies reporting low knob numbers in NF or even knob-less NF lines, the European flints contained similar knob numbers to the US dents<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 41" title="Brown, W. L. Numbers and distribution of chromosome knobs in United States maize. Genetics 34, 524‚Äì536 (1949)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0671-9#ref-CR41" id="ref-link-section-d28078e1549">41</a></sup>. It is unclear whether this is a characteristic of the NF ancestral lines migrated to Europe, reflects the breeding history in European flints or represents a technical advantage given the progress in methodology to detect weaker signals. Consistent with fiber-FISH studies, we also detected numerous locations of the 180-bp knob repeat unit throughout the chromosome assemblies<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 42" title="Adawy, S. S., Stupar, R. M. &amp; Jiang, J. Fluorescence in situ hybridization analysis reveals multiple loci of knob-associated DNA elements in one-knob and knobless maize lines. J. Histochem. Cytochem. 52, 1113‚Äì1116 (2004)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0671-9#ref-CR42" id="ref-link-section-d28078e1553">42</a></sup>. The potential contribution of these widespread knob repeats on gene density, expression levels and rare deleterious alleles requires future studies and adds further complexity to known effects of large-scale knobs in maize on recombination rates, meiotic drive and phenotypic traits such as flowering time<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 26" title="Ghaffari, R. et al. Maize chromosomal knobs are located in gene-dense areas and suppress local recombination. Chromosoma 122, 67‚Äì75 (2013)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0671-9#ref-CR26" id="ref-link-section-d28078e1557">26</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 43" title="Jian, Y. et al. Maize (Zea mays L.) genome size indicated by 180-bp knob abundance is associated with flowering time. Sci. Rep. 7, 5954 (2017)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0671-9#ref-CR43" id="ref-link-section-d28078e1560">43</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 44" title="Kanizay, L. B. et al. Intragenomic conflict between the two major knob repeats of maize. Genetics 194, 81‚Äì89 (2013)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0671-9#ref-CR44" id="ref-link-section-d28078e1563">44</a></sup>.</p><p>WGAs enabled us to identify long runs of pairwise and higher order combinations of genomic regions with identical haplotypes. Size and distribution of these haplotype blocks reflect complexity of historic recombination, intercrossing and breeding history over the last centuries<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 3" title="Chia, J. M. et al. Maize HapMap2 identifies extant variation from a genome in flux. Nat. Genet 44, 803‚Äì807 (2012)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0671-9#ref-CR3" id="ref-link-section-d28078e1570">3</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 5" title="Anderson, E. &amp; Brown, W. L. The History of the Common Maize Varieties of the United States Corn Belt (Univ. of California Press, 1952)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0671-9#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d28078e1573">5</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 7" title="van Heerwaarden, J., Hufford, M. B. &amp; Ross-Ibarra, J. Historical genomics of North American maize. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 109, 12420‚Äì12425 (2012)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0671-9#ref-CR7" id="ref-link-section-d28078e1576">7</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 19" title="Unterseer, S. et al. A comprehensive study of the genomic differentiation between temperate Dent and Flint maize. Genome Biol. 17, 137 (2016)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0671-9#ref-CR19" id="ref-link-section-d28078e1579">19</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Hufford, M. B. et al. Comparative population genomics of maize domestication and improvement. Nat. Genet. 44, 808 (2012)." href="#ref-CR45" id="ref-link-section-d28078e1582">45</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Lai, J. et al. Genome-wide patterns of genetic variation among elite maize inbred lines. Nat. Genet. 42, 1027 (2010)." href="#ref-CR46" id="ref-link-section-d28078e1582_1">46</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 47" title="Reif, J. C. et al. Trends in genetic diversity among European maize cultivars and their parental components during the past 50 years. Theor. Appl. Genet. 111, 838‚Äì845 (2005)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0671-9#ref-CR47" id="ref-link-section-d28078e1585">47</a></sup>. Similar to the gene and repeat comparisons, the SNP runs highlight the separation of flints and dents of this study but also illustrate and delineate candidate regions of common ancestry in the US Corn Belt and European flint lines. We identified a set of regions that differentiated the studied Corn Belt dents from the European germplasm. Intriguingly, genes differentially expressed between two groups of maize varieties that have been selected by their genotypic similarity to these regions comprised a nearly complete starch biosynthesis pathway and several genes involved in the establishment and organization of the endosperm texture. Hence, besides the detection of selective sweeps and other genomic signatures, it might be equally informative to survey <i>trans</i>-effects of such genomic regions.</p><p>Given the high dynamics and tessellation of the maize genome, we estimate that many high-quality genome sequences will be required for improving breeding efficacy and understanding maize biology. The four European flint lines of this study close an important gap in our knowledge and complement the maize pangenome.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec11" data-title="Methods"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec11-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec11">Methods</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec11-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec12">Plant material and genome assembly</h3><p>The four flint inbred lines were chosen to represent landraces from different European ancestry. While PE0075 (a doubled-haploid line derived from the landrace Petkuser Ferdinand Rot) and DK105 (derived from Gelber Badischer Landmais) can be classified as a representatives of the NF, EP1 and F7 represent Pyrenean‚ÄìGalician ancestry and were derived from populations Lizargarate and Lacaune, respectively<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 48" title="Mayer, M. et al. Is there an optimum level of diversity in utilization of genetic resources? Theor. Appl. Genet. 130, 2283‚Äì2295 (2017)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0671-9#ref-CR48" id="ref-link-section-d28078e1607">48</a></sup>. Contigs, scaffolds and pseudochromosomes were assembled de novo using the DeNovoMAGIC 2.0 technology for lines EP1 and F7, and DeNovoMAGIC 3.0 for lines DK105 and PE0075 (Energin R Technologies 2009), respectively (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-0671-9#MOESM1">2</a>) using the B73 (v.4) reference genome to assist anchoring and orienting scaffolds and contigs to pseudochromosomes. The application of this toolset has been described previously for several plant genome assemblies<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 15" title="Hirsch, C. N. et al. Draft assembly of elite inbred line PH207 provides insights into genomic and transcriptome diversity in maize. Plant Cell 28, 2700‚Äì2714 (2016)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0671-9#ref-CR15" id="ref-link-section-d28078e1614">15</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 16" title="Springer, N. M. et al. The maize W22 genome provides a foundation for functional genomics and transposon biology. Nat. Genet. 50, 1282‚Äì1288 (2018)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0671-9#ref-CR16" id="ref-link-section-d28078e1617">16</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 49" title="Avni, R. et al. Wild emmer genome architecture and diversity elucidate wheat evolution and domestication. Science 357, 93‚Äì97 (2017)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0671-9#ref-CR49" id="ref-link-section-d28078e1620">49</a></sup>. Briefly, bulked leaf tissue from 15‚Äì24 seedlings was harvested, immediately ground in liquid nitrogen and genomic DNA was then isolated using a modified CTAB protocol<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 50" title="Saghai-Maroof, M. A. et al. Ribosomal DNA spacer-length polymorphisms in barley: Mendelian inheritance, chromosomal location, and population dynamics. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 81, 8014‚Äì8018 (1984)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0671-9#ref-CR50" id="ref-link-section-d28078e1624">50</a></sup>. Illumina sequencing of paired-end and mate-pair libraries was performed as specified in Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-0671-9#MOESM1">1</a>. Remappings of the paired-end and mate-pair libraries were used to estimate gap sizes in the assemblies (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-0671-9#MOESM1">10</a>).</p><p>A cross of parental lines PH207‚Äâ√ó‚ÄâEP1 was used to establish a high-density genetic map from an F<sub>2</sub> mapping population. Parents and 192 F<sub>2</sub> plants were analyzed using the Affymetrix Axiom Maize Genotyping Array and data were processed as described in ref. <sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 31" title="Unterseer, S. et al. A powerful tool for genome analysis in maize: development and evaluation of the high density 600 k SNP genotyping array. BMC Genomics 15, 823 (2014)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0671-9#ref-CR31" id="ref-link-section-d28078e1642">31</a></sup>, which resulted in 174,616 markers. The markers were clustered into groups showing identical segregation patterns using POPSEQ<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 51" title="Mascher, M. et al. Anchoring and ordering NGS contig assemblies by population sequencing (POPSEQ). Plant J. 76, 718‚Äì727 (2013)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0671-9#ref-CR51" id="ref-link-section-d28078e1646">51</a></sup>. A first genetic map was calculated with 9,404 binmap markers with the R package ASMap v.1.0-2 (ref. <sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 52" title="Taylor, J. &amp; Butler, D. R package ASMap: efficient genetic linkage map construction and diagnosis. J. Stat. Softw. 79, 1‚Äì29 (2017)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0671-9#ref-CR52" id="ref-link-section-d28078e1650">52</a></sup>) using the function mstmap with the following parameters: pop.type=‚ÄúRIL2‚Äù, dist.fun=‚Äúkosambi‚Äù, objective.fun=‚ÄúCOUNT‚Äù, p.value=1e-22, noMap.dist=15, noMap.size=2, miss.thresh=0.00. Linkage groups were assigned to the ten maize chromosomes on the basis of previously mapped markers. Unlinked small groups with only a few markers were discarded. Ten F<sub>2</sub> plants exhibited very high numbers of crossovers and were excluded from further analyses. In multiple rounds of mapping using the same parameters as stated above, markers with highly distorted segregation or which led to double-crossovers were identified using the function statMark in the ASMap R package and discarded before final map construction. The final genetic linkage map contained 8,869 markers. All markers from the initial dataset which had a Hamming distance of 0 with one of the mapped binmap markers were inserted into the map, resulting in a genetic map with 174,071 markers.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec13">Gene annotation and consolidation</h3><p>We predicted protein-coding structures for F7 and EP1 as consensus models using an approach as previously described<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 53" title="Paterson, A. H. et al. The Sorghum bicolor genome and the diversification of grasses. Nature 457, 551‚Äì556 (2009)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0671-9#ref-CR53" id="ref-link-section-d28078e1665">53</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 54" title="Wang, W. et al. The Spirodela polyrhiza genome reveals insights into its neotenous reduction fast growth and aquatic lifestyle. Nat. Commun. 5, 3311 (2014)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0671-9#ref-CR54" id="ref-link-section-d28078e1668">54</a></sup>. Briefly, consensus gene models are based on transcriptome evidences from F7 and EP1 RNA-seq data generated within this study (see <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="section anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-0671-9#Sec18">RNA preparation</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="section anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-0671-9#Sec19">Expression analysis</a>) and protein homologies of known monocotyledonous protein sequences (maize B73 and PH207, <i>Sorghum bicolor</i> v.3.1., <i>Oryza sativa</i> v.7 MSU and <i>Brachypodium distachyon</i> v.3.1)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 53" title="Paterson, A. H. et al. The Sorghum bicolor genome and the diversification of grasses. Nature 457, 551‚Äì556 (2009)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0671-9#ref-CR53" id="ref-link-section-d28078e1688">53</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 55" title="Kawahara, Y. et al. Improvement of the Oryza sativa Nipponbare reference genome using next generation sequence and optical map data. Rice 6, 4 (2013)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0671-9#ref-CR55" id="ref-link-section-d28078e1691">55</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 56" title="The International Brachypodium Initiative Genome sequencing and analysis of the model grass Brachypodium distachyon. Nature 463, 763‚Äì768 (2010)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0671-9#ref-CR56" id="ref-link-section-d28078e1694">56</a></sup>. Evidences were mapped onto the genome assembly applying GenomeThreader<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 57" title="Gremme, G. et al. Engineering a software tool for gene structure prediction in higher organisms. Inf. Softw. Technol. 47, 965‚Äì978 (2005)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0671-9#ref-CR57" id="ref-link-section-d28078e1698">57</a></sup> using a minimum alignment coverage of 50% and seed sizes 7‚Äì10 for protein and 18 for nucleic acid matches. Before mapping, transcript data were assembled using Trinity with default parameters and Bridger with <i>K</i>-mer sizes 25 and 29‚Äâbp (refs. <sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 58" title="Chang, Z. et al. Bridger: a new framework for de novo transcriptome assembly using RNA-seq data. Genome Biol. 16, 30 (2015)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0671-9#ref-CR58" id="ref-link-section-d28078e1705">58</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 59" title="Grabherr, M. G. et al. Full-length transcriptome assembly from RNA-Seq data without a reference genome. Nat. Biotechnol. 29, 644‚Äì652 (2011)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0671-9#ref-CR59" id="ref-link-section-d28078e1708">59</a></sup>). Subsequently, these sequences were combined by the evidential gene pipeline (<a href="http://arthropods.eugenes.org/EvidentialGene">http://arthropods.eugenes.org/EvidentialGene</a>) to obtain the final EP1 and F7 transcriptome assemblies. Initial consensus gene models were subsequently consolidated as described in the <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-0671-9#MOESM1">Supplementary Notes</a> to derive final gene sets for the four flints and two dent lines.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec14">FISH and karyotyping</h3><p>Maize chromosomes were prepared from root meristems of 2-day-old seedlings. Roots were cut and treated with 2‚ÄâmM of 8-hydroxyquinoline solution for 3.5‚Äâh. Fixation was performed overnight at room temperature in 3:1 (ethanol:acetic acid) fixative. Slides were prepared according to ref. <sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 60" title="Aliyeva-Schnorr, L., Ma, L. &amp; Houben, A. A fast air-dry dropping chromosome preparation method suitable for fish in plants. J. Vis. Exp. 106, 53470 (2015)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0671-9#ref-CR60" id="ref-link-section-d28078e1728">60</a></sup> and in situ hybridization was performed as described in ref. <sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 61" title="Badaeva, E. D. et al. in Fluorescence In Situ Hybridization (FISH): Application Guide (ed. Liehr, T.) 477‚Äì494 (Springer, 2017)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0671-9#ref-CR61" id="ref-link-section-d28078e1732">61</a></sup>. Oligonucleotide probes for karyotyping and identification of knob repeats were chosen from ref. <sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 62" title="Zhu, M. et al. A simple and efficient nondenaturing FISH method for maize chromosome differentiation using single-strand oligonucleotide probes. Genome 60, 657‚Äì664 (2017)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0671-9#ref-CR62" id="ref-link-section-d28078e1736">62</a></sup>.</p><p>Images were taken using an Olympus BX61 microscope equipped with an ORCA-ER CCD camera (Hamamatsu). All images were acquired in gray scale and pseudocoloured with Adobe Photoshop CS5 (Adobe Systems). Karyotyping was done according to ref. <sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 62" title="Zhu, M. et al. A simple and efficient nondenaturing FISH method for maize chromosome differentiation using single-strand oligonucleotide probes. Genome 60, 657‚Äì664 (2017)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0671-9#ref-CR62" id="ref-link-section-d28078e1743">62</a></sup> using the maize line B73 as a reference.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec15">Repeat analysis</h3><p>To obtain equivalent transposon and tandem repeat data for comparative analyses we annotated all six lines with the same annotation workflows. An homology search against the Panicoidae section of the PGSB transposon library<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 63" title="Spannagl, M. et al. PGSB PlantsDB: updates to the database framework for comparative plant genome research. Nucleic Acids Res. 44, D1141‚ÄìD1147 (2016)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0671-9#ref-CR63" id="ref-link-section-d28078e1755">63</a></sup> resulted in a basal transposon annotation. The REdat_9.8_Panicoideae used contains publicly available Panicoideae transposons templates as well as de novo detected full-length LTR-retrotranspons from maize (12,510 elements from B73 (v.2)) and sorghum (3,368 elements). The program vmatch (<a href="http://www.vmatch.de">http://www.vmatch.de</a>) was used as a fast and efficient matching tool well suited for large and highly repetitive genomes under the following parameter setup: identity ‚â•70%, minimal hit length 75‚Äâbp, seedlength 12‚Äâbp (exact commandline: -d -p -l 75 -identity 70 -seedlength 12 -exdrop 5). The vmatch output was filtered for redundant hits via a priority-based approach, which assigns higher scoring matches first and either shortens (&lt;90% coverage and ‚â•50-bp rest length) or removes lower scoring overlaps. The resulting annotation is free of overlaps. Elements that have been interrupted by other transposon insertions (nesting) are not defragmented into a higher order instance (such as exons belonging to one gene).</p><p>Full-length LTR-retrotransposons (fl-LTR) where identified with LTRharvest<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 64" title="Ellinghaus, D., Kurtz, S. &amp; Willhoeft, U. LTRharvest, an efficient and flexible software for de novo detection of LTR retrotransposons. BMC Bioinformatics 9, 18 (2008)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0671-9#ref-CR64" id="ref-link-section-d28078e1766">64</a></sup> using the following parameters: overlaps best -seed 30 -minlenltr 100 -maxlenltr 2000 -mindistltr 3000 -maxdistltr 25000 -similar 85 -mintsd 4 -maxtsd 20 -motif tgca -motifmis 1 -vic 60 -xdrop 5 -mat 2 -mis -2 -ins -3 -del -3. All candidates from the LTRharvest output were subsequently annotated with PfamA domains using hmmer3 (<a href="http://hmmer.org">http://hmmer.org</a>) and stringently filtered for false positives by several criteria, the main ones being the presence of at least one typical retrotransposon domain (for example, reverse transcriptase (RT), RNase H (RH), integrase (INT), protease (PR) and so on) and a tandem repeat content &lt;25%. The inner domain order served as a criterion for the classification into the Gypsy (RT‚ÄìRH‚ÄìINT) or Copia (INT‚ÄìRT‚ÄìRH) superfamily abbreviated as RLG and RLC. Elements missing either INT or RT were classified as RLX. The insertion age of each full-length LTR-retrotransposon was estimated on the basis of the accumulated divergence between its 5‚Ä≤ and 3‚Ä≤ LTRs and a random mutation rate of 1.3‚Äâ√ó‚Äâ10<sup>‚Äì8</sup> (ref. <sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 65" title="SanMiguel, P. et al. The paleontology of intergene retrotransposons of maize. Nat. Genet. 20, 43‚Äì45 (1998)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0671-9#ref-CR65" id="ref-link-section-d28078e1776">65</a></sup>).</p><p>Tandem repeats were identified with the TandemRepeatFinder under default parameters<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 66" title="Benson, G. Tandem repeats finder: a program to analyze DNA sequences. Nucleic Acids Res. 27, 573‚Äì580 (1999)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0671-9#ref-CR66" id="ref-link-section-d28078e1783">66</a></sup> and subjected to an overlap removal as described above, prioritizing longer and higher scoring elements. <i>K</i>-mer frequencies were calculated with Tallymer<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 67" title="Kurtz, S. et al. A new method to compute K-mer frequencies and its application to annotate large repetitive plant genomes. BMC Genomics 9, 517 (2008)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0671-9#ref-CR67" id="ref-link-section-d28078e1790">67</a></sup>.</p><p>Syntentic fl-LTRs where identified by sequence clustering (vmatch dbcluster, 98% identity and 98% coverage) of TE junctions from the de novo annotated fl-LTR locations from each line. The junctions consisted of 2‚Äâ√ó‚Äâ100-bp sequence signatures spanning the upstream and downstream insertion sites with each 50‚Äâbp inside and 50‚Äâbp outside of the TE element. The LTR assembly index was calculated using the LTR_retriever program suite<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 68" title="Ou, S. &amp; Jiang, N. LTR_retriever: a highly accurate and sensitive program for identification of long terminal repeat retrotransposons. Plant Physiol. 176, 1410‚Äì1422 (2018)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0671-9#ref-CR68" id="ref-link-section-d28078e1797">68</a></sup> as suggested on the Github pages of LTR_retriever (<a href="https://github.com/oushujun/LTR_retriever">github.com/oushujun/LTR_retriever</a>). LTR_FINDER (ref. <sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 69" title="Xu, Z. &amp; Wang, H. LTR_FINDER: an efficient tool for the prediction of full-length LTR retrotransposons. Nucleic Acids Res. 35, W265‚ÄìW268 (2007)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0671-9#ref-CR69" id="ref-link-section-d28078e1805">69</a></sup>) was run via the wrapper LTR_FINDER_parallel<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 70" title="Ou, S. &amp; Jiang, N. LTR_FINDER_parallel: parallelization of LTR_FINDER enabling rapid identification of long terminal repeat retrotransposons. Mob. DNA 10, 48 (2019)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0671-9#ref-CR70" id="ref-link-section-d28078e1809">70</a></sup> using the following parameters: -w 2 -C -D 15000 -d 1000 -L 7000 -l 100 -p 20 -M 0.85 (20 threads and 5-Mb batches). LTRHarvest<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 64" title="Ellinghaus, D., Kurtz, S. &amp; Willhoeft, U. LTRharvest, an efficient and flexible software for de novo detection of LTR retrotransposons. BMC Bioinformatics 9, 18 (2008)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0671-9#ref-CR64" id="ref-link-section-d28078e1813">64</a></sup> was run with: -minlenltr 100 -maxlenltr 7000 -mintsd 4 -maxtsd 6 -motif TGCA -motifmis 1 -similar 85 -vic 10 -seed 20.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec16">Whole-genome alignments</h3><p>Block alignments of high identity were generated for all pairwise combinations of the six maize lines using the MUMMER v.3 suite<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 71" title="Kurtz, S. et al. Versatile and open software for comparing large genomes. Genome Biol. 5, R12 (2004)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0671-9#ref-CR71" id="ref-link-section-d28078e1826">71</a></sup>. Initial alignments were computed using nucmer with a minimal cluster size of 250‚Äâbp and a seed size of 20‚Äâbp. Results were piped through the delta-filter tool selecting the best one-to-one blocks (global option -1) with a minimal size of 500‚Äâbp (-l 500). Despite this filter step, many (~41,000‚Äì81,000) putatively translocated blocks aligned small regions between different chromosomes or unanchored scaffolds. These blocks frequently overlapped ‚Äòregular‚Äô blocks linking same orthologous chromosomes and showed significantly lower sequence identities in comparison to their respective regular blocks with which they overlapped. Hence, they likely represented paralogous alignments triggered by tandem repeats or the absence of the truly orthologous sequences in one of the lines. To derive the final set of SAB, we only included candidate interchromosomal translocations with a minimal sequence identity of 99% and a maximum overlap of 10‚Äâbp to its adjacent alignments. To gain an overview of the contiguity between the six maize genomes, SABs were connected to MABs if SABs were directly adjacent in both genomes with a consistent orientation (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-0671-9#MOESM1">11</a>).</p><p>To identify and delineate genomic regions for each line that aligned (1) to none of the other lines (designated as ‚Äòunaligned‚Äô), (2) to all lines of the same group (either flint or dent maize) and to none of the other group (aka ‚Äògroup-specific) or (3) to all five other lines (‚Äòcore6‚Äô), we superimposed SABs and MABs of one line on its genome coordinates by recording from which group and how many times a single genomic base participated in an alignment. Thereby, we classified each base and concatenated adjacent bases of the same type (1‚Äì3) to derive the unaligned, group-specific and core genomic part. Reversing this approach, identified core regions of EP1 were reprojected onto the pairwise alignments to decode the sequence and coordinates of matching core elements in the other five lines. On the basis of these coordinates, sequences for each core block were extracted and aligned applying the Fast Sequence Aligner<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 72" title="Bradley, R. K. et al. Fast statistical alignment. PLoS Comput. Biol. 5, e1000392 (2009)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0671-9#ref-CR72" id="ref-link-section-d28078e1836">72</a></sup> with default parameters.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec17">Determination of paired and higher order haplotypes</h3><p>SNPs were directly determined from multiple sequence alignments of the WGA core blocks (omitting insertions/deletions). Their position in each of the six genomes was derived from the position in the alignment and the known genomic start- and end-positions of the respective core block. Given the observed SNP frequencies, and applying randomization studies and run-of-head statistics, the expected maximal run lengths of identical SNPs between two lines range from 21 to 30 consecutive SNPs. We selected genomic windows of ‚â•40 identical SNPs as seeds to identify shared haplotypes of likely common ancestry and used those as seeds for a greedy extension to delineate genomic segments with ‚â•98% sequence identity between all 15 pairwise line combinations. To deduce higher order haplotypes, we transformed the 15 pairwise runs-of-identities to binary SNP patterns and surveyed seeds with ‚â•40 consecutive identical binary configurations. An iterative greedy algorithm linking the seed with adjacent upstream and downstream runs extended such seeds if the run exhibited a haplotype identical to the seed and less than three nonmatching SNPs were observed between the current end of the extension and the candidate run.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec18">RNA preparation</h3><p>Different tissues of the European maize (<i>Z. mays</i>) inbred lines EP1 and F7 were sampled according to refs. <sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 73" title="Downs, G. S. et al. A developmental transcriptional network for maize defines coexpression modules. Plant Physiol. 161, 1830‚Äì1843 (2013)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0671-9#ref-CR73" id="ref-link-section-d28078e1859">73</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 74" title="Sekhon, R. S. et al. Genome-wide atlas of transcription during maize development. Plant J. 66, 553‚Äì563 (2011)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0671-9#ref-CR74" id="ref-link-section-d28078e1862">74</a></sup> and subsequently subjected to transcriptome sequencing (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-0671-9#MOESM1">5</a>). Samples 1‚Äì3 were collected from seeds imbibed (whole seed, sample 1) or germinated (primary root, sample 2; coleoptile, sample 3) in paper rolls<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 75" title="Hetz, W. et al. Isolation and characterization of rtcs, a maize mutant deficient in the formation of nodal roots. Plant J. 10, 845‚Äì857 (1996)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0671-9#ref-CR75" id="ref-link-section-d28078e1869">75</a></sup> in a 16‚Äâh light (28‚Äâ¬∞C) and 8‚Äâh dark (21‚Äâ¬∞C) regime of a growth cabinet (Conviron CMP6010, <a href="http://www.conviron.com">http://www.conviron.com</a>). Tissue samples 4‚Äì24 were taken from plants grown in a climate chamber either in small pots (14‚Äâcm top diameter, 8‚Äâcm height, 0.25‚Äâl volume for samples 4‚Äì17) or in big pots (28‚Äâcm top diameter, 21‚Äâcm height, 10‚Äâl volume for samples 18‚Äì24) containing soil substrate type ED 73 (<a href="https://www.meyer-shop.com">https://www.meyer-shop.com</a>). Growth conditions in the climate chamber were 16‚Äâh light (28‚Äâ¬∞C) and 8‚Äâh dark (21‚Äâ¬∞C). Two individual plants were collected for each of the 24 tissues. Harvested plant material was immediately frozen in liquid nitrogen and stored at ‚àí80‚Äâ¬∞C until RNA extraction. For total RNA extraction, the 24 samples per genotype were separately ground in liquid nitrogen. Subsequently, one spatula of each of the 24 samples per genotype was mixed to generate one pool per inbred line EP1 and F7, respectively. Total RNA was extracted from each of the two pools using the Qiagen RNeasy mini kit according to the manufacturer‚Äôs protocol (Qiagen, <a href="https://www.qiagen.com">https://www.qiagen.com</a>), including on-column DNA digestion. RNA quality was determined by agarose gel electrophoresis and by a Bioanalyzer using an Agilent RNA 6000 Nano Chip (Agilent Technologies, <a href="https://www.agilent.com">https://www.agilent.com</a>). Both RNA samples were of excellent quality with RNA integrity number values<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 76" title="Schroeder, A. et al. The RIN: an RNA integrity number for assigning integrity values to RNA measurements. BMC Mol. Biol. 7, 3 (2006)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0671-9#ref-CR76" id="ref-link-section-d28078e1890">76</a></sup> between 9.8 and 10. In total, 9‚Äâ¬µg of total RNA per pool were used for RNA-seq at Novogene (Novogene). After mRNA enrichment using oligo(dT) beads, sequencing libraries were constructed with the NEBNext Ultra RNA Library Prep Kit for Illumina (New England Biolabs) and Illumina PE (150‚Äâbp‚Äâ√ó‚Äâ2) sequencing was performed on a HiSeq4000 by Novogene. The number of raw reads and quality-filtered reads are shown in Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-0671-9#MOESM1">12</a>. Reads containing adapters, reads containing &gt;10% undefined bases and reads containing &gt;50% low quality bases (Qscore 5) were removed.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec19">Expression analysis and analysis of DEGs</h3><p>Genomic variants overlapping the flint/dent specific haplotype identified in this study were extracted from the Maize Hapmap v.3.2.1 panel<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 34" title="Bukowski, R. et al. Construction of the third-generation Zea mays haplotype map. Gigascience 7, 1‚Äì12 (2018)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0671-9#ref-CR34" id="ref-link-section-d28078e1905">34</a></sup> for lines of the 282 inbred maize association panel<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 77" title="Flint-Garcia, S. A. et al. Maize association population: a high-resolution platform for quantitative trait locus dissection. Plant J. 44, 1054‚Äì1064 (2005)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0671-9#ref-CR77" id="ref-link-section-d28078e1909">77</a></sup>. Selected variants were subjected to phylogenetic analysis using FastTree (v.2.1.5 SSE3)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 78" title="Price, M. N., Dehal, P. S. &amp; Arkin, A. P. FastTree 2‚Äîapproximately maximum-likelihood trees for large alignments. PLoS ONE 5, e9490 (2010)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0671-9#ref-CR78" id="ref-link-section-d28078e1913">78</a></sup> with default parameters and the resulting phylogenetic tree was visualized using iTOL<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 79" title="Letunic, I. &amp; Bork, P. Interactive Tree Of Life (iTOL) v4: recent updates and new developments. Nucleic Acids Res. 47, W256‚ÄìW259 (2019)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0671-9#ref-CR79" id="ref-link-section-d28078e1917">79</a></sup> (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-0671-9#MOESM1">18</a>). Two nonoverlapping subtrees were selected such that three of the lines presented in this study (B73, EP1 and F7) are contained, together with other topologically close lines. All lines in the subtree containing B73 were assigned to group one; all those in the subtree containing both EP1 and F7 were assigned to group two.</p><p>For the resulting subset of 41 lines (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-0671-9#MOESM1">9</a>) samples from seven different tissues (germinating shoot and root, the tip and base of leaves as well as leaf samples under light and dark conditions and kernels) were downloaded from SRA (<a href="https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/bioproject/?term=PRJNA383416">PRJNA383416</a>)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 30" title="Kremling, K. A. G. et al. Dysregulation of expression correlates with rare-allele burden and fitness loss in maize. Nature 555, 520 (2018)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0671-9#ref-CR30" id="ref-link-section-d28078e1934">30</a></sup>. Read preprocessing was performed as described in the Lexogen QuantSeq user guide (<a href="https://www.lexogen.com/wp-content/uploads/2018/10/015UG108V0201-QuantSeq-Data-Analysis-Pipeline.pdf">https://www.lexogen.com/wp-content/uploads/2018/10/015UG108V0201-QuantSeq-Data-Analysis-Pipeline.pdf</a>). Trimmed reads were mapped to the maize reference sequences B73 (v.4), EP1 and F7 using STAR aligner v.2.5.1a (ref. <sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 80" title="Dobin, A. et al. STAR: ultrafast universal RNA-seq aligner. Bioinformatics 29, 15‚Äì21 (2013)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0671-9#ref-CR80" id="ref-link-section-d28078e1942">80</a></sup>) applying parameters outFilterMultimapNmax=10, outFilterMismatchNoverLmax=0.04,outFilterIntronMotifs=RemoveNoncanonicalUnannotated To obtain nonunique gene-level counts from the mapping files, HTSeq (v.0.11.2) with the ‚Äònonunique all‚Äô-method was used<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 81" title="Anders, S., Pyl, P. T. &amp; Huber, W. HTSeq‚Äîa Python framework to work with high-throughput sequencing data. Bioinformatics 31, 166‚Äì169 (2015)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0671-9#ref-CR81" id="ref-link-section-d28078e1947">81</a></sup>. Normalization of read counts was performed by library sequence depth using the R package DESeq2 (v.1.23.3)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 82" title="Love, M. I., Huber, W. &amp; Anders, S. Moderated estimation of fold change and dispersion for RNA-seq data with DESeq2. Genome Biol. 15, 550 (2014)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0671-9#ref-CR82" id="ref-link-section-d28078e1951">82</a></sup>. For differential gene expression analysis with DESeq2, lines classified into either the flint- or dent-like group were treated as biological replicates. In this study, we report only genes for which all three orthologs of RNA-seq read mappings to B73, EP1 and F7 were significant at Benjamini‚ÄìHochberg adjusted <i>P</i>‚Äâ‚â§‚Äâ0.05.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec20">Reporting Summary</h3><p>Further information on research design is available in the <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-0671-9#MOESM2">Nature Research Reporting Summary</a> linked to this article.</p></div></div></section>
                

                <section aria-labelledby="data-availability" data-title="Data availability"><div class="c-article-section" id="data-availability-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="data-availability">Data availability</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="data-availability-content">
              
              <p>The de novo assembled genomes and raw reads were released in collaboration with the Maize Genetics and Genomics Database MaizeGDB<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 18" title="Yang, N. et al. Genome assembly of a tropical maize inbred line provides insights into structural variation and crop improvement. Nat. Genet. 51, 1052‚Äì1059 (2019)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0671-9#ref-CR18" id="ref-link-section-d28078e2056">18</a></sup> and NCBI BioProjects <a href="https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/bioproject/?term=PRJNA360923">PRJNA360923</a> (F7, DK105 and PE0075) and <a href="https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/bioproject/?term=PRJNA360920">PRJNA360920</a> (EP1). Additionally, RNA-seq raw data were also deposited under these NCBI accessions.</p>

Cumulative plot, lower curves represent higher repetitivity e.g. 50% of the PH207 assembly consists of 20mers occurring‚Äâ&lt;‚Äâ=10 times, for the other lines this value is only around 40%. <b>c</b>, Transposon content.</p></div></div><div class="c-article-supplementary__item js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="supp-item" id="Fig7"><h3 class="c-article-supplementary__title u-h3"><a class="print-link" data-track="click" data-track-action="view supplementary info" data-track-label="link" data-test="supp-info-link" href="/articles/s41588-020-0671-9/figures/7" data-supp-info-image="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/esm/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41588-020-0671-9/MediaObjects/41588_2020_671_Fig7_ESM.jpg">Extended Data Fig. 2 Genetic versus physical distances for all ten chromosomes of the EP1xPH207 cross.</a></h3><div class="c-article-supplementary__description" data-component="thumbnail-container"><p>X-axis: physical position on the respective EP1 chromosome in [Mb], Y-axis: genetic position in centiMorgan [cM].</p></div></div><div class="c-article-supplementary__item js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="supp-item" id="Fig8"><h3 class="c-article-supplementary__title u-h3"><a class="print-link" data-track="click" data-track-action="view supplementary info" data-track-label="link" data-test="supp-info-link" href="/articles/s41588-020-0671-9/figures/8" data-supp-info-image="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/esm/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41588-020-0671-9/MediaObjects/41588_2020_671_Fig8_ESM.jpg">Extended Data Fig. 3 Group-specific syntelogs for all (4,2)-combinations of the six lines and their syntelog set.</a></h3><div class="c-article-supplementary__description" data-component="thumbnail-container"><p>We divided the six maize lines of this study into two groups of 4 (gray circles) and 2 (white circles) lines, respectively. For all 15 combinations, the number of group-specific syntelogs for each group was determined under the prior of the identified orthologous gene set. The germplasm-specific separation of flint (EP1, F7, DK105 and PE0075) versus dent (B73 version 4 and PH207) genotypes showed the largest distinction between the two groups.</p></div></div><div class="c-article-supplementary__item js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="supp-item" id="Fig9"><h3 class="c-article-supplementary__title u-h3"><a class="print-link" data-track="click" data-track-action="view supplementary info" data-track-label="link" data-test="supp-info-link" href="/articles/s41588-020-0671-9/figures/9" data-supp-info-image="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/esm/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41588-020-0671-9/MediaObjects/41588_2020_671_Fig9_ESM.jpg">Extended Data Fig. 4 Expression levels of orthologs with contiguous and disrupted open reading frame (ORF).</a></h3><div class="c-article-supplementary__description" data-component="thumbnail-container"><p>Consolidated mappings discovered several thousand genes for which the entire cross-mapping of the top-scoring consolidation model had no contiguous but a disrupted ORF. These genes exhibit highly similar expression levels to their nondisrupted orthologs illustrated here for lines EP1 and F7. <b>a</b>, shows the control correlation and linear regression (red line) of a random sample of F7 and EP1 orthologous pairs for which both genes have a contiguous ORF. To obtain comparable sample sizes and expression ranges, random sampling was restricted to gene pairs with a maximal TPM‚Äâ‚â§‚Äâ100. <b>b</b>, and <b>c</b>, display expression correlations between gene pairs with exactly one disrupted and one contiguous ORF, while in subfigure <b>d</b>, both orthologous partners contain a disrupted ORF. N: number of pairs, r2: coefficient of determination.</p></div></div><div class="c-article-supplementary__item js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="supp-item" id="Fig10"><h3 class="c-article-supplementary__title u-h3"><a class="print-link" data-track="click" data-track-action="view supplementary info" data-track-label="link" data-test="supp-info-link" href="/articles/s41588-020-0671-9/figures/10" data-supp-info-image="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/esm/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41588-020-0671-9/MediaObjects/41588_2020_671_Fig10_ESM.jpg">Extended Data Fig. 5 Full-length LTR-retrotransposon detection in different maize lines.</a></h3><div class="c-article-supplementary__description" data-component="thumbnail-container"><p><b>a</b>, <i>De novo</i> detection and stringent quality filtering of fl-LTR elements. <b>b</b>, Retrieved fl-LTR numbers as proxy for assembly quality. Upper data points give candidate fl-LTRs, lower data points give filtered, high-quality fl-LTR numbers. Five of the six assemblies contain the expected number of fl-LTRs. The by 50% reduced numbers of high-quality elements in PH207 reflect an older not optimized assembly approach. <b>c</b>, Insertion age distribution of fl-LTRs. The four NRGene assemblies have very similar age distribution patterns. The B73 assembly has a higher proportion of very young Copia and Gypsy elements, which can probably be attributed to technical assembly differences. In PH207, the young elements are completely missing in the assembly and thus explain the reduced numbers. Due to their (almost) identical long terminal repeats the correct structure of young elements is more difficult to resolve.</p></div></div><div class="c-article-supplementary__item js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="supp-item" id="Fig11"><h3 class="c-article-supplementary__title u-h3"><a class="print-link" data-track="click" data-track-action="view supplementary info" data-track-label="link" data-test="supp-info-link" href="/articles/s41588-020-0671-9/figures/11" data-supp-info-image="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/esm/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41588-020-0671-9/MediaObjects/41588_2020_671_Fig11_ESM.jpg">Extended Data Fig. 6 Tandem repeat content and composition in the genome assemblies.</a></h3><div class="c-article-supplementary__description" data-component="thumbnail-container"><p><b>a</b>, The assemblies of EP1 and F7 captured the highest amounts of tandem repeats and knob sequences. The in spite of high fish intensities much lower contents in the B73 and PH207 assemblies reflect the assembly difficulties of these highly repetitive genome regions. <b>b</b>, Relationship between the six lines derived from knob location similarities. The input matrix for clustering contained three relative intensities of fish knob locations per chromosome. The knob patterns of the two Dents B73 and PH207 are very similar, as well as the patterns of DK105 and PE0075. <b>c</b>, Multiple sequence alignment of selected knob monomers from EP1 together with known knob monomers. The knob sequences in the EP1 assembly consist of 180 and 202‚Äâbp monomers with a surplus of the 180‚Äâbp monomer by a factor of 6.8. Both monomers are highly similar to previously reported knob monomers from maize with the following Genbank IDs marked as 1, 2, 3 in the figure, 1: <a href="https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/nuccore/AF030934.1">AF030934.1</a>, 2: <a href="https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/nuccore/M32521.1">M32521.1</a> and <a href="https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/nuccore/M32525.1">M32525.1</a>, 3: <a href="https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/nuccore/DQ352544">DQ352544</a>.1_a and <a href="https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/nuccore/DQ352544">DQ352544</a>.1_b. Consensus sequences of the monomers where used to identify all major and minor knob locations in the assemblies. <b>d</b>, Gene expression (maximal expression of 7 different conditions per Gene, log10) in relation to the nearest upstream knob signature (left) and tandem repeat (right). Both axes are logarithmic.</p></div></div><div class="c-article-supplementary__item js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="supp-item" id="Fig12"><h3 class="c-article-supplementary__title u-h3"><a class="print-link" data-track="click" data-track-action="view supplementary info" data-track-label="link" data-test="supp-info-link" href="/articles/s41588-020-0671-9/figures/12" data-supp-info-image="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/esm/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41588-020-0671-9/MediaObjects/41588_2020_671_Fig12_ESM.jpg">Extended Data Fig. 7 Syntenic knob signatures.</a></h3><div class="c-article-supplementary__description" data-component="thumbnail-container"><p>x-axis: number of knob units per 2‚ÄâMb windows, y axis: chromosome length in Mb. <b>a</b>, <b>Minor knob locations</b> in the assemblies (y max=100). The majority of the minor knob locations are syntenic between the 8 lines, especially for chromosomes 4,5,and 6, and display no clear distinction between flint and dent. This is in contrast to the knob intensities and extent detected by FISH hybridizations (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-0671-9#Fig3">3a</a>). We hypothesize that these minor locations act as ‚Äòanchor points‚Äô for knob movement or expansion, which seem to occur within very short evolutionary time-spans since the separation of different breeding lines. Larger knob stretches could impact the expression of surrounding genes and influence local recombination rates. <b>b</b>, <b>Major knob locations</b> in the assemblies (Y max=4000).</p></div></div><div class="c-article-supplementary__item js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="supp-item" id="Fig13"><h3 class="c-article-supplementary__title u-h3"><a class="print-link" data-track="click" data-track-action="view supplementary info" data-track-label="link" data-test="supp-info-link" href="/articles/s41588-020-0671-9/figures/13" data-supp-info-image="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/esm/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41588-020-0671-9/MediaObjects/41588_2020_671_Fig13_ESM.jpg">Extended Data Fig. 8 Densities of the core, group-specific and unaligned regions for the six maize lines studies.</a></h3><div class="c-article-supplementary__description" data-component="thumbnail-container"><p>Intensities are normalized within one track, dark red/blue values representing a higher fraction of aligned regions per 5‚ÄâMb bins. Outer chromosome ideograms show the largest of all six lines, dark gray ellipses indicate the approximate centromere position derived from the repeat analysis. Group of six circular tracks show from outer to inner the core, germplasm-specific and unaligned densities. Each group consists of six tracks, displaying from outer to inner densities for B73, PH207, EP1, F7, DK105 and PE0075.</p></div></div><div class="c-article-supplementary__item js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="supp-item" id="Fig14"><h3 class="c-article-supplementary__title u-h3"><a class="print-link" data-track="click" data-track-action="view supplementary info" data-track-label="link" data-test="supp-info-link" href="/articles/s41588-020-0671-9/figures/14" data-supp-info-image="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/esm/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41588-020-0671-9/MediaObjects/41588_2020_671_Fig14_ESM.jpg">Extended Data Fig. 9 Genomic sizes in [Mb] of SNP runs-of-identity (RoI) for all 15 pairwise comparisons of the six maize lines.</a></h3><div class="c-article-supplementary__description" data-component="thumbnail-container"><p>Comparisons within one germplasm (flint: EP1, F7, DK105 and PE0075; dent: B73 version 4 and PH207) share larger genomic fractions than inter-germplasm RoI totals. Y-axis: total sum of RoIs in [Mb], X-axis: line pairs, gray circles indicate maize line used in the respective comparison.</p></div></div></div></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec23" data-title="Supplementary information"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec23-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec23">Supplementary information</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec23-content"><div data-test="supplementary-info">
